<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hard_versus_soft">Hard versus soft</span></h2>
<p>It is common to classify wood as either <a href="/wiki/Softwood" title="Softwood">softwood</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hardwood" title="Hardwood">hardwood</a>. The wood from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pinophyta" title="Pinophyta">conifers</a> (e.g. pine) is called softwood, and the wood from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dicotyledons" title="Dicotyledons">dicotyledons</a> (usually broad-leaved trees, e.g. oak) is called hardwood. These names are a bit misleading, as hardwoods are not necessarily hard, and softwoods are not necessarily soft. The well-known balsa (a hardwood) is actually softer than any commercial softwood. Conversely, some softwoods (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Taxus_baccata" title="Taxus baccata">yew</a>) are harder than many hardwoods.
</p><p>There is a strong relationship between the properties of wood and the properties of the particular tree that yielded it, at least for certain species. For example, in loblolly pine, wind exposure and stem position greatly affect the hardness of wood, as well as compression wood content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The density of wood varies with species. The density of a wood correlates with its strength (mechanical properties). For example, <a href="/wiki/Mahogany" title="Mahogany">mahogany</a> is a medium-dense hardwood that is excellent for fine furniture crafting, whereas <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ochroma_pyramidale" title="Ochroma pyramidale">balsa</a> is light, making it useful for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_model" title="Physical model">model</a> building. One of the densest woods is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olea_laurifolia" title="Olea laurifolia">black ironwood</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lignin.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2559" data-file-width="4828" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Lignin.png/300px-Lignin.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Lignin.png/450px-Lignin.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Lignin.png/600px-Lignin.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lignin.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chemical structure of <a href="/wiki/Lignin" title="Lignin">lignin</a>, which makes up about 25% of wood dry matter and is responsible for many of its properties.</div></div></div>
<p>The chemical composition of wood varies from species to species, but is approximately 50% carbon, 42% oxygen, 6% hydrogen, 1% nitrogen, and 1% other elements (mainly <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>) by weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Wood also contains <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, and other elements in small quantity.
</p><p>Aside from water, wood has three main components. <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">Cellulose</a>, a crystalline polymer derived from glucose, constitutes about 41–43%. Next in abundance is <a href="/wiki/Hemicellulose" title="Hemicellulose">hemicellulose</a>, which is around 20% in deciduous trees but near 30% in conifers. It is mainly <a href="/wiki/Pentose" title="Pentose">five-carbon sugars</a> that are linked in an irregular manner, in contrast to the cellulose. <a href="/wiki/Lignin" title="Lignin">Lignin</a> is the third component at around 27% in coniferous wood vs. 23% in deciduous trees. Lignin confers the hydrophobic properties reflecting the fact that it is based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aromatic_ring" title="Aromatic ring">aromatic rings</a>. These three components are interwoven, and direct covalent linkages exist between the lignin and the hemicellulose. A major focus of the paper industry is the separation of the lignin from the cellulose, from which paper is made.
</p><p>In chemical terms, the difference between hardwood and softwood is reflected in the composition of the constituent <a href="/wiki/Lignin" title="Lignin">lignin</a>. Hardwood lignin is primarily derived from <a href="/wiki/Sinapyl_alcohol" title="Sinapyl alcohol">sinapyl alcohol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coniferyl_alcohol" title="Coniferyl alcohol">coniferyl alcohol</a>. Softwood lignin is mainly derived from coniferyl alcohol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boerjan_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-boerjan-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extractives">Extractives</span></h3>
<p>Aside from the structural <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymers</a>, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemicellulose" title="Hemicellulose">hemicellulose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lignin" title="Lignin">lignin</a> (<a href="/wiki/Lignocellulosic_biomass" title="Lignocellulosic biomass">lignocellulose</a>), wood contains a large variety of non-structural constituents, composed of low <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_weight" title="Molecular weight">molecular weight</a> <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a>, called <i>extractives</i>. These compounds are present in the <a href="/wiki/Extracellular_space" title="Extracellular space">extracellular space</a> and can be extracted from the wood using different neutral <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvents</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Acetone" title="Acetone">acetone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book1_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Book1-31">[31]</a></sup> Analogous content is present in the so-called <i>exudate</i> produced by trees in response to mechanical damage or after being attacked by <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book2_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Book2-32">[32]</a></sup> Unlike the structural constituents, the composition of extractives varies over wide ranges and depends on many factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The amount and composition of extractives differs between tree species, various parts of the same tree, and depends on genetic factors and growth conditions, such as climate and geography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book1_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Book1-31">[31]</a></sup> For example, slower growing trees and higher parts of trees have higher content of extractives. Generally, the <a href="/wiki/Softwood" title="Softwood">softwood</a> is richer in extractives than the <a href="/wiki/Hardwood" title="Hardwood">hardwood</a>. Their concentration increases from the <a href="/wiki/Cambium" title="Cambium">cambium</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Pith" title="Pith">pith</a>. <a href="/wiki/Bark_(botany)" title="Bark (botany)">Barks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Branch" title="Branch">branches</a> also contain extractives. Although extractives represent a small fraction of the wood content, usually less than 10%, they are extraordinarily diverse and thus characterize the chemistry of the wood species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book3_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Book3-34">[34]</a></sup> Most extractives are secondary metabolites and some of them serve as precursors to other chemicals. Wood extractives display different activities, some of them are produced in response to wounds, and some of them participate in natural defense against insects and fungi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Forchem_Rauma_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3460" data-file-width="5005" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Forchem_Rauma_2.jpg/220px-Forchem_Rauma_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Forchem_Rauma_2.jpg/330px-Forchem_Rauma_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Forchem_Rauma_2.jpg/440px-Forchem_Rauma_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Forchem_Rauma_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Forchem <a href="/wiki/Tall_oil" title="Tall oil">tall oil</a> refinery in <a href="/wiki/Rauma,_Finland" title="Rauma, Finland">Rauma</a>, Finland.</div></div></div>
<p>These compounds contribute to various physical and chemical properties of the wood, such as wood color, fragnance, durability, acoustic properties, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hygroscopicity" title="Hygroscopicity">hygroscopicity</a>, adhesion, and drying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book3_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Book3-34">[34]</a></sup> Considering these impacts, wood extractives also affect the properties of <a href="/wiki/Pulp_(paper)" title="Pulp (paper)">pulp</a> and paper, and importantly cause many problems in <a href="/wiki/Papermaking" title="Papermaking">paper industry</a>. Some extractives are surface-active substances and unavoidably affect the surface properties of paper, such as water adsorption, friction and strength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book1_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Book1-31">[31]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lipophilicity" title="Lipophilicity">Lipophilic</a> extractives often give rise to sticky deposits during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kraft_pulping" title="Kraft pulping">kraft pulping</a> and may leave spots on paper. Extractives also account for paper smell, which is important when making <a href="/wiki/Food_contact_materials" title="Food contact materials">food contact materials</a>.
</p><p>Most wood extractives are <a href="/wiki/Lipophilicity" title="Lipophilicity">lipophilic</a> and only a little part is water-soluble.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book2_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Book2-32">[32]</a></sup> The lipophilic portion of extractives, which is collectively referred as wood <a href="/wiki/Resin" title="Resin">resin</a>, contains <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sterol" title="Sterol">sterols</a> and steryl esters, <a href="/wiki/Terpene" title="Terpene">terpenes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terpenoid" title="Terpenoid">terpenoids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Resin_acid" title="Resin acid">resin acids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wax" title="Wax">waxes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The heating of resin, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a>, vaporizes the <a href="/wiki/Volatility_(chemistry)" title="Volatility (chemistry)">volatile</a> terpenes and leaves the solid component – <a href="/wiki/Rosin" title="Rosin">rosin</a>. The concentrated liquid of volatile compounds extracted during <a href="/wiki/Steam_distillation" title="Steam distillation">steam distillation</a> is called <a href="/wiki/Essential_oil" title="Essential oil">essential oil</a>. Distillation of <a href="/wiki/Oleoresin" title="Oleoresin">oleoresin</a> obtained from many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pines" title="Pines">pines</a> provides <a href="/wiki/Rosin" title="Rosin">rosin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turpentine" title="Turpentine">turpentine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most extractives can be categorized into three groups: <a href="/wiki/Aliphatic_compound" title="Aliphatic compound">aliphatic compounds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terpene" title="Terpene">terpenes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phenols" title="Phenols">phenolic compounds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book1_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-Book1-31">[31]</a></sup> The latter are more water-soluble and usually are absent in the resin.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Aliphatic_compound" title="Aliphatic compound">Aliphatic compounds</a> include fatty acids, <a href="/wiki/Fatty_alcohol" title="Fatty alcohol">fatty alcohols</a> and their esters with <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a>, fatty alcohols (waxes) and sterols (steryl esters). <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">Hydrocarbons</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkanes</a>, are also present in the wood. <a href="/wiki/Suberin" title="Suberin">Suberin</a> is a polyester, made of suberin acids and glycerol, mainly found in <a href="/wiki/Bark_(botany)" title="Bark (botany)">barks</a>. Fats serve as a source of energy for the wood cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book2_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Book2-32">[32]</a></sup> The most common wood sterol is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sitosterol" title="Sitosterol">sitosterol</a>. However, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sitostanol" title="Sitostanol">sitostanol</a>, citrostadienol, <a href="/wiki/Campesterol" title="Campesterol">campesterol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a> are also observed both in the hardwood and softwood, although in low quantities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book1_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-Book1-31">[31]</a></sup></li>
<li>The main <a href="/wiki/Terpene" title="Terpene">terpenes</a> occurring in the softwood include <a href="/wiki/Monoterpene" title="Monoterpene">mono-</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sesquiterpene" title="Sesquiterpene">sesqui-</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diterpene" title="Diterpene">diterpenes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book2_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-Book2-32">[32]</a></sup> Meanwhile, the terpene composition of the hardwood is considerably different, consisting of <a href="/wiki/Triterpene#Triterpenoids" title="Triterpene">triterpenoids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polyprenol" title="Polyprenol">polyprenols</a> and other higher terpenes. Examples of mono-, di- and sesquiterpenes are <a href="/wiki/Alpha-Pinene" title="Alpha-Pinene">α-</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beta-Pinene" title="Beta-Pinene">β-pinenes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3-carene" title="3-carene">3-carene</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%92-myrcene" title="Β-myrcene">β-myrcene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Limonene" title="Limonene">limonene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thujaplicin" title="Thujaplicin">thujaplicins</a>, α- and β-<a href="/wiki/Phellandrene" title="Phellandrene">phellandrenes</a>, α-muurolene, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%94-cadinene" title="Δ-cadinene">δ-cadinene</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha-cadinol" title="Alpha-cadinol">α-</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delta-cadinol" title="Delta-cadinol">δ-cadinols</a>, α- and β-<a href="/wiki/Cedrene" title="Cedrene">cedrenes</a>, juniperol, <a href="/wiki/Longifolene" title="Longifolene">longifolene</a>, <i>cis</i>-abienol, <a href="/wiki/Borneol" title="Borneol">borneol</a>, pinifolic acid, nootkatin, chanootin, <a href="/wiki/Phytol" title="Phytol">phytol</a>, geranyl-linalool, β-epimanool, manoyloxide, pimaral and pimarol. Resin acids are usually <a href="/wiki/Tricyclic" title="Tricyclic">tricyclic</a> <a href="/wiki/Terpenoid" title="Terpenoid">terpenoids</a>, examples of which are <a href="/wiki/Pimaric_acid" title="Pimaric acid">pimaric acid</a>, sandaracopimaric acid, <a href="/wiki/Isopimaric_acid" title="Isopimaric acid">isopimaric acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abietic_acid" title="Abietic acid">abietic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Levopimaric_acid" title="Levopimaric acid">levopimaric acid</a>, palustric acid, neoabietic acid and dehydroabietic acid. <a href="/wiki/Bicyclic_molecule" title="Bicyclic molecule">Bicyclic</a> resin acids are also found, such as lambertianic acid, communic acid, mercusic acid and secodehydroabietic acid. <a href="/wiki/Cycloartenol" title="Cycloartenol">Cycloartenol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Betulin" title="Betulin">betulin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Squalene" title="Squalene">squalene</a> are <a href="/wiki/Triterpene#Triterpenoids" title="Triterpene">triterpenoids</a> purified from hardwood. Examples of wood polyterpenes are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rubber" title="Rubber">rubber</a> (<i>cis</i>-polypren), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gutta_percha" title="Gutta percha">gutta percha</a> (<i>trans</i>-polypren), gutta-balatá (<i>trans</i>-polypren) and betulaprenols (<a href="/wiki/Open-chain_compound" title="Open-chain compound">acyclic</a> polyterpenoids).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book1_31-5"><a href="#cite_note-Book1-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book2_32-4"><a href="#cite_note-Book2-32">[32]</a></sup> The mono- and sesquiterpenes of the softwood are responsible for the typical smell of <a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">pine</a> forest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book1_31-6"><a href="#cite_note-Book1-31">[31]</a></sup> Many monoterpenoids, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%92-myrcene" title="Β-myrcene">β-myrcene</a>, are used in the preparation of <a href="/wiki/Flavoring" title="Flavoring">flavors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perfume" title="Perfume">fragrances</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book2_32-5"><a href="#cite_note-Book2-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tropolone" title="Tropolone">Tropolones</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Hinokitiol" title="Hinokitiol">hinokitiol</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Thujaplicin" title="Thujaplicin">thujaplicins</a>, are present in <a href="/wiki/Wood-decay_fungus" title="Wood-decay fungus">decay</a>-resistant trees and display <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungicidal" title="Fungicidal">fungicidal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Insecticide" title="Insecticide">insecticidal</a> properties. Tropolones strongly bind metal ions and can cause digester <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a> in the process <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kraft_pulping" title="Kraft pulping">kraft pulping</a>. Owing to their <a href="/wiki/Chelation" title="Chelation">metal-binding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ionophore" title="Ionophore">ionophoric</a> properties, especially thujaplicins are used in physiology experiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Different other <i>in-vitro</i> biological activities of thujaplicins have been studied, such as insecticidal, anti-browning, anti-viral, anti-bacterial, anti-fungal, anti-proliferative and anti-oxidant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Phenols" title="Phenols">Phenolic compounds</a> are especially found in the hardwood and the bark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book2_32-6"><a href="#cite_note-Book2-32">[32]</a></sup> The most well-known wood phenolic constituents are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stilbenes" title="Stilbenes">stilbenes</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Pinosylvin" title="Pinosylvin">pinosylvin</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lignans" title="Lignans">lignans</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Pinoresinol" title="Pinoresinol">pinoresinol</a>, conidendrin, <a href="/wiki/Plicatic_acid" title="Plicatic acid">plicatic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydroxymatairesinol" title="Hydroxymatairesinol">hydroxymatairesinol</a>), norlignans (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Nyasol" title="Nyasol">nyasol</a>, puerosides A and B, hydroxysugiresinol, sequirin-C), <a href="/wiki/Tannin" title="Tannin">tannins</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Gallic_acid" title="Gallic acid">gallic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ellagic_acid" title="Ellagic acid">ellagic acid</a>), <a href="/wiki/Flavonoid" title="Flavonoid">flavonoids</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Chrysin" title="Chrysin">chrysin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taxifolin" title="Taxifolin">taxifolin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catechin" title="Catechin">catechin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genistein" title="Genistein">genistein</a>). Most of the phenolic compounds have fungicidal properties and protect the wood from <a href="/wiki/Wood-decay_fungus" title="Wood-decay fungus">fungal decay</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book2_32-7"><a href="#cite_note-Book2-32">[32]</a></sup> Together with the neolignans the phenolic compounds influence on the color of the wood. Resin acids and phenolic compounds are the main toxic contaminants present in the untreated <a href="/wiki/Effluent" title="Effluent">effluents</a> from <a href="/wiki/Pulp_(paper)" title="Pulp (paper)">pulping</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book1_31-7"><a href="#cite_note-Book1-31">[31]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyphenolic" title="Polyphenolic">Polyphenolic</a> compounds are one of the most abundant biomolecules produced by plants, such as <a href="/wiki/Flavonoid" title="Flavonoid">flavonoids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tannin" title="Tannin">tannins</a>. Tannins are used in <a href="/wiki/Leather" title="Leather">leather</a> industry and have shown to exhibit different biological activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book3_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Book3-34">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Flavonoid" title="Flavonoid">Flavonoids</a> are very diverse, widely distributed in the <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plant</a> kingdom and have numerous biological activities and roles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book2_32-8"><a href="#cite_note-Book2-32">[32]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel">Fuel</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wood_fuel" title="Wood fuel">Wood fuel</a></div>
<p>Wood has a long history of being used as fuel,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> which continues to this day, mostly in rural areas of the world. Hardwood is preferred over softwood because it creates less smoke and burns longer. Adding a woodstove or fireplace to a home is often felt to add ambiance and warmth.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pulpwood">Pulpwood</span></h3>
<p>Pulpwood is wood that is raised specifically for use in making paper.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Construction">Construction</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saitta_House_Dyker_Heights.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Saitta_House_Dyker_Heights.JPG/170px-Saitta_House_Dyker_Heights.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Saitta_House_Dyker_Heights.JPG/255px-Saitta_House_Dyker_Heights.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Saitta_House_Dyker_Heights.JPG/340px-Saitta_House_Dyker_Heights.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saitta_House_Dyker_Heights.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Saitta_House" title="Saitta House">Saitta House</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dyker_Heights" title="Dyker Heights">Dyker Heights</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn" title="Brooklyn">Brooklyn</a>, New York built in 1899 is made of and decorated in wood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Wood has been an important construction material since humans began building shelters, houses and boats. Nearly all boats were made out of wood until the late 19th century, and wood remains in common use today in boat construction. <a href="/wiki/Elm" title="Elm">Elm</a> in particular was used for this purpose as it resisted decay as long as it was kept wet (it also served for water pipe before the advent of more modern plumbing).
</p><p>Wood to be used for construction work is commonly known as <i><a href="/wiki/Lumber" title="Lumber">lumber</a></i> in North America. Elsewhere, <i>lumber</i> usually refers to felled trees, and the word for sawn planks ready for use is <i>timber</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> In Medieval Europe <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a> was the wood of choice for all wood construction, including beams, walls, doors, and floors. Today a wider variety of woods is used: solid wood doors are often made from <a href="/wiki/Populus" title="Populus">poplar</a>, small-knotted <a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">pine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Douglas_fir" title="Douglas fir">Douglas fir</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%86%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%8C_(%D0%B4%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%B2%D1%8F%D0%BD%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%8F)_(1714_%D0%B3.)_01.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%86%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%8C_%28%D0%B4%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%B2%D1%8F%D0%BD%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%8F%29_%281714_%D0%B3.%29_01.JPG/220px-%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%86%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%8C_%28%D0%B4%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%B2%D1%8F%D0%BD%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%8F%29_%281714_%D0%B3.%29_01.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%86%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%8C_%28%D0%B4%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%B2%D1%8F%D0%BD%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%8F%29_%281714_%D0%B3.%29_01.JPG/330px-%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%86%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%8C_%28%D0%B4%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%B2%D1%8F%D0%BD%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%8F%29_%281714_%D0%B3.%29_01.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%86%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%8C_%28%D0%B4%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%B2%D1%8F%D0%BD%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%8F%29_%281714_%D0%B3.%29_01.JPG/440px-%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%86%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%8C_%28%D0%B4%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%B2%D1%8F%D0%BD%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%8F%29_%281714_%D0%B3.%29_01.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%86%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%8C_(%D0%B4%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%B2%D1%8F%D0%BD%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%8F)_(1714_%D0%B3.)_01.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The churches of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kizhi" title="Kizhi">Kizhi</a>, Russia are among a handful of <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Sites</a> built entirely of wood, without metal joints. See <a href="/wiki/Kizhi_Pogost" title="Kizhi Pogost">Kizhi Pogost</a> for more details.</div></div></div>
<p>New domestic housing in many parts of the world today is commonly made from timber-framed construction. <a href="/wiki/Engineered_wood" title="Engineered wood">Engineered wood</a> products are becoming a bigger part of the construction industry. They may be used in both residential and commercial buildings as structural and aesthetic materials.
</p><p>In buildings made of other materials, wood will still be found as a supporting material, especially in roof construction, in interior doors and their frames, and as exterior cladding.
</p><p>Wood is also commonly used as shuttering material to form the mold into which concrete is poured during <a href="/wiki/Reinforced_concrete" title="Reinforced concrete">reinforced concrete</a> construction.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flooring">Flooring</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LightningVolt_Wood_Floor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="447" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/LightningVolt_Wood_Floor.jpg/220px-LightningVolt_Wood_Floor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/LightningVolt_Wood_Floor.jpg/330px-LightningVolt_Wood_Floor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/LightningVolt_Wood_Floor.jpg/440px-LightningVolt_Wood_Floor.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LightningVolt_Wood_Floor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wood can be cut into straight planks and made into a <a href="/wiki/Wood_flooring" title="Wood flooring">wood flooring</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wood_flooring" title="Wood flooring">Wood flooring</a></div><p>A solid wood floor is a floor laid with planks or battens created from a single piece of timber, usually a hardwood. Since wood is hydroscopic (it acquires and loses moisture from the ambient conditions around it) this potential instability effectively limits the length and width of the boards.
</p><p>Solid hardwood flooring is usually cheaper than engineered timbers and damaged areas can be sanded down and refinished repeatedly, the number of times being limited only by the thickness of wood above the tongue.
</p><p>Solid hardwood floors were originally used for structural purposes, being installed perpendicular to the wooden support beams of a building (the joists or bearers) and solid construction timber is still often used for sports floors as well as most traditional wood blocks, <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parquetry" title="Parquetry">parquetry</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Engineered_products">Engineered products</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Engineered_wood" title="Engineered wood">Engineered wood</a></div>
<p>Engineered wood products, glued building products "engineered" for application-specific performance requirements, are often used in construction and industrial applications. Glued engineered wood products are manufactured by bonding together wood strands, veneers, lumber or other forms of wood fiber with glue to form a larger, more efficient composite structural unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apawood_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-apawood-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>These products include <a href="/wiki/Glued_laminated_timber" title="Glued laminated timber">glued laminated timber</a> (glulam), wood structural panels (including <a href="/wiki/Plywood" title="Plywood">plywood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oriented_strand_board" title="Oriented strand board">oriented strand board</a> and composite panels), <a href="/wiki/Laminated_veneer_lumber" title="Laminated veneer lumber">laminated veneer lumber</a> (LVL) and other structural composite lumber (SCL) products, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parallel_strand_lumber" title="Parallel strand lumber">parallel strand lumber</a>, and I-joists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apawood_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-apawood-44">[44]</a></sup> Approximately 100 million cubic meters of wood was consumed for this purpose in 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-4">[4]</a></sup> The trends suggest that particle board and fiber board will overtake plywood.
</p><p>Wood unsuitable for construction in its native form may be broken down mechanically (into fibers or chips) or chemically (into cellulose) and used as a raw material for other building materials, such as engineered wood, as well as <a href="/wiki/Particle_board" title="Particle board">chipboard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hardboard" title="Hardboard">hardboard</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medium-density_fiberboard" title="Medium-density fiberboard">medium-density fiberboard</a> (MDF). Such wood derivatives are widely used: wood fibers are an important component of most paper, and cellulose is used as a component of some <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound#Synthetic_compounds" title="Organic compound">synthetic materials</a>. Wood derivatives can be used for kinds of flooring, for example <a href="/wiki/Laminate_flooring" title="Laminate flooring">laminate flooring</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Furniture_and_utensils">Furniture and utensils</span></h3>
<p>Wood has always been used extensively for <a href="/wiki/Furniture" title="Furniture">furniture</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chairs" title="Chairs">chairs</a> and beds. It is also used for tool handles and cutlery, such as <a href="/wiki/Chopsticks" title="Chopsticks">chopsticks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toothpick" title="Toothpick">toothpicks</a>, and other utensils, like the <a href="/wiki/Wooden_spoon" title="Wooden spoon">wooden spoon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pencil" title="Pencil">pencil</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h3>
<p>Further developments include new <a href="/wiki/Lignin" title="Lignin">lignin</a> glue applications, recyclable food packaging, rubber tire replacement applications, anti-bacterial medical agents, and high strength fabrics or composites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
As scientists and engineers further learn and develop new techniques to extract various components from wood, or alternatively to modify wood, for example by adding components to wood, new more advanced products will appear on the marketplace. Moisture content electronic monitoring can also enhance next generation wood protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prayer_Bead_with_the_Adoration_of_the_Magi_and_the_Crucifixion_MET_DP371962.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3404" data-file-width="2603" decoding="async" height="288" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Prayer_Bead_with_the_Adoration_of_the_Magi_and_the_Crucifixion_MET_DP371962.jpg/220px-Prayer_Bead_with_the_Adoration_of_the_Magi_and_the_Crucifixion_MET_DP371962.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Prayer_Bead_with_the_Adoration_of_the_Magi_and_the_Crucifixion_MET_DP371962.jpg/330px-Prayer_Bead_with_the_Adoration_of_the_Magi_and_the_Crucifixion_MET_DP371962.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Prayer_Bead_with_the_Adoration_of_the_Magi_and_the_Crucifixion_MET_DP371962.jpg/440px-Prayer_Bead_with_the_Adoration_of_the_Magi_and_the_Crucifixion_MET_DP371962.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prayer_Bead_with_the_Adoration_of_the_Magi_and_the_Crucifixion_MET_DP371962.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Prayer_Bead_with_the_Adoration_of_the_Magi_and_the_Crucifixion" title="Prayer Bead with the Adoration of the Magi and the Crucifixion">Prayer Bead with the Adoration of the Magi and the Crucifixion</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Gothic_boxwood_miniature" title="Gothic boxwood miniature">Gothic boxwood miniature</a></div></div></div>
<p>Wood has long been used as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Media_(arts)" title="Media (arts)">artistic medium</a>. It has been used to make sculptures and <a href="/wiki/Wood_carving" title="Wood carving">carvings</a> for millennia. Examples include the <a href="/wiki/Totem_pole" title="Totem pole">totem poles</a> carved by North American indigenous people from conifer trunks, often Western Red Cedar (<i><a href="/wiki/Thuja_plicata" title="Thuja plicata">Thuja plicata</a></i>).
</p><p>Other uses of wood in the arts include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Woodcut" title="Woodcut">Woodcut</a> <a href="/wiki/Printmaking" title="Printmaking">printmaking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wood_engraving" title="Wood engraving">engraving</a></li>
<li>Wood can be a surface to paint on, such as in <a href="/wiki/Panel_painting" title="Panel painting">panel painting</a></li>
<li>Many <a href="/wiki/Musical_instrument" title="Musical instrument">musical instruments</a> are made mostly or entirely of wood</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports_and_recreational_equipment">Sports and recreational equipment</span></h3>
<p>Many types of <a href="/wiki/Sports_equipment" title="Sports equipment">sports equipment</a> are made of wood, or were constructed of wood in the past. For example, <a href="/wiki/Cricket_bat" title="Cricket bat">cricket bats</a> are typically made of <a href="/wiki/Salix_alba" title="Salix alba">white willow</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Baseball_bat" title="Baseball bat">baseball bats</a> which are legal for use in <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a> are frequently made of <a href="/wiki/Fraxinus" title="Fraxinus">ash wood</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hickory" title="Hickory">hickory</a>, and in recent years have been constructed from <a href="/wiki/Maple" title="Maple">maple</a> even though that wood is somewhat more fragile. <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association" title="National Basketball Association">National Basketball Association</a> courts have been traditionally made out of <a href="/wiki/Parquetry#Use_in_the_NBA" title="Parquetry">parquetry</a>.
</p><p>Many other types of sports and recreation equipment, such as <a href="/wiki/Ski" title="Ski">skis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_stick" title="Ice hockey stick">ice hockey sticks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lacrosse_stick" title="Lacrosse stick">lacrosse sticks</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bow_(weapon)" title="Bow (weapon)">archery bows</a>, were commonly made of wood in the past, but have since been replaced with more modern materials such as aluminium, <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Composite_material" title="Composite material">composite materials</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Fiberglass" title="Fiberglass">fiberglass</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_fiber" title="Carbon fiber">carbon fiber</a>. One noteworthy example of this trend is the family of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golf_club_(equipment)" title="Golf club (equipment)">golf clubs</a> commonly known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Wood_(golf)" title="Wood (golf)">woods</a></i>, the heads of which were traditionally made of <a href="/wiki/Diospyros" title="Diospyros">persimmon</a> wood in the early days of the game of golf, but are now generally made of metal or (especially in the case of <a href="/wiki/Wood_(golf)#Drivers" title="Wood (golf)">drivers</a>) carbon-fiber composites.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bacterial_degradation">Bacterial degradation</span></h2>
<p>Little is known about the bacteria that degrade cellulose. <a href="/wiki/Symbiotic_bacteria" title="Symbiotic bacteria">Symbiotic bacteria</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Xylophaga" title="Xylophaga">Xylophaga</a></i> may play a role in the degradation of sunken wood. <i><a href="/wiki/Alphaproteobacteria" title="Alphaproteobacteria">Alphaproteobacteria</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flavobacteria" title="Flavobacteria">Flavobacteria</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Actinomycetota" title="Actinomycetota">Actinomycetota</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Clostridia" title="Clostridia">Clostridia</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Bacteroidota" title="Bacteroidota">Bacteroidota</a></i> have been detected in wood submerged for over a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>
